         <th scope="row">Signature</th>
         <td><a href="/wiki/File:Signature_of_Gordon_Brown.png" class="image" title="Gordon Brown's signature"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/24/Signature_of_Gordon_Brown.png/128px-Signature_of_Gordon_Brown.png" decoding="async" width="128" height="27" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/24/Signature_of_Gordon_Brown.png/192px-Signature_of_Gordon_Brown.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/24/Signature_of_Gordon_Brown.png/256px-Signature_of_Gordon_Brown.png 2x" data-file-width="907" data-file-height="192"></a></td>

      <p><b>James Gordon Brown</b> <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Honorary_Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Edinburgh" class="mw-redirect" title="Honorary Fellow of the Royal Society of Edinburgh">HonFRSE</a></span> (born 20 February 1951) is a British politician who was <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Labour Party (UK)">Leader of the Labour Party</a> from <a href="/wiki/2007_Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election" class="mw-redirect" title="2007 Labour Party (UK) leadership election">2007</a> to <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2010 United Kingdom general election">2010</a>. He served as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> from <a href="/wiki/1997_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1997 United Kingdom general election">1997</a> to <a href="/wiki/2007_Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election" class="mw-redirect" title="2007 Labour Party (UK) leadership election">2007</a>. Brown was a <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament_(United_Kingdom)" title="Member of Parliament (United Kingdom)">Member of Parliament</a> (MP) from <a href="/wiki/1983_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1983 United Kingdom general election">1983</a> to <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2015 United Kingdom general election">2015</a>, first for <a href="/wiki/Dunfermline_East_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Dunfermline East (UK Parliament constituency)">Dunfermline East</a> and later for <a href="/wiki/Kirkcaldy_and_Cowdenbeath_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Kirkcaldy and Cowdenbeath (UK Parliament constituency)">Kirkcaldy and Cowdenbeath</a>. </p>
      <p>A doctoral graduate of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a>, Brown spent his early career working as both a lecturer at a <a href="/wiki/Further_education" title="Further education">further education</a> college and a television journalist. He entered Parliament in <a href="/wiki/1983_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1983 United Kingdom general election">1983</a> as the MP for Dunfermline East. He joined the <a href="/wiki/Official_Opposition_Shadow_Cabinet_(United_Kingdom)" title="Official Opposition Shadow Cabinet (United Kingdom)">Shadow Cabinet</a> in 1989 as <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Business,_Innovation_and_Skills" title="Department for Business, Innovation and Skills">Shadow Secretary of State for Trade</a>, and was later promoted to become <a href="/wiki/Shadow_Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Shadow Chancellor of the Exchequer">Shadow Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> in 1992. After Labour's victory in <a href="/wiki/1997_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1997 United Kingdom general election">1997</a>, he was appointed Chancellor of the Exchequer, becoming the longest-serving holder of that office in modern history. </p>
      <p>Brown's time as Chancellor was marked by major reform of Britain's monetary and fiscal policy architecture, transferring interest rate setting powers to the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a>, by a wide extension of the powers of the <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">Treasury</a> to cover much domestic policy and by transferring responsibility for banking supervision to the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Services_Authority" title="Financial Services Authority">Financial Services Authority</a>. Controversial moves included the abolition of <a href="/wiki/Advance_corporation_tax" title="Advance corporation tax">advance corporation tax</a> (ACT) relief in his first budget,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and the removal in his final budget of the <a href="/wiki/10p_tax_rate" title="10p tax rate">10% "starting rate"</a> of personal income tax which he had introduced in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-10pence_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10pence-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> resigned as Prime Minister and Labour Leader in 2007, and Brown was chosen to replace him in <a href="/wiki/2007_Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election" class="mw-redirect" title="2007 Labour Party (UK) leadership election">an uncontested election</a>. </p>
      <p>After initial rises in opinion polls following Brown becoming Prime Minister, Labour's popularity declined with the onset of a <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">recession in 2008</a>, leading to poor results in the <a href="/wiki/2009_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2009 United Kingdom local elections">local</a> and <a href="/wiki/2009_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="2009 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">European elections in 2009</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-timesonline1_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timesonline1-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-labour_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-labour-6">[6]</a></sup> A year later, Labour lost 91 seats in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> at the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2010 United Kingdom general election">2010 general election</a>, the party's biggest loss of seats in a single general election since <a href="/wiki/1931_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1931 United Kingdom general election">1931</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> making the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a> the largest party in a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a>. Brown remained in office as Labour negotiated to form a coalition government with the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>. On 10 May 2010, Brown announced he would stand down as leader of the Labour Party, and instructed the party to put into motion the processes to elect a new leader. Labour's attempts to retain power failed and on 11 May, he officially resigned as Prime Minister and Leader of the Labour Party. He was succeeded as Prime Minister by <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, and as Leader of the Labour Party by <a href="/wiki/Ed_Miliband" title="Ed Miliband">Ed Miliband</a>. </p>
      <p>Later, Brown played a prominent role in the campaign to maintain the union during the <a href="/wiki/2014_Scottish_independence_referendum" title="2014 Scottish independence referendum">2014 Scottish independence referendum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2> 
      <p>Brown was born at the Orchard Maternity Nursing Home in <a href="/wiki/Giffnock" title="Giffnock">Giffnock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renfrewshire_(historic)" title="Renfrewshire (historic)">Renfrewshire</a>, Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> His father was John Ebenezer Brown (1914–1998), a <a href="/wiki/Ministers_and_elders_of_the_Church_of_Scotland" title="Ministers and elders of the Church of Scotland">minister of the Church of Scotland</a> and a strong influence on Brown.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> His mother was Jessie Elizabeth "Bunty" Brown (<a href="/wiki/Given_name#Name_at_birth" title="Given name"><span title="Name at birth">née</span></a> Souter; 1918–2004).<sup id="cite_ref-mother_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mother-12">[12]</a></sup> She was the daughter of John Souter, a timber merchant.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The family moved to <a href="/wiki/Kirkcaldy" title="Kirkcaldy">Kirkcaldy</a> – then the largest town in <a href="/wiki/Fife" title="Fife">Fife</a>, across the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a> from <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a><sup id="cite_ref-aaa_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aaa-14">[14]</a></sup> – when Gordon was three.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Brown was brought up there with his elder brother John and younger brother <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Brown_(media_strategist)" title="Andrew Brown (media strategist)">Andrew Brown</a><sup id="cite_ref-mother_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mother-12">[12]</a></sup> in a <a href="/wiki/Manse" title="Manse">manse</a>; he is therefore often referred to as a "son of the manse", an idiomatic Scottish phrase.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3> 
      <p>Brown was educated first at Kirkcaldy West Primary School where he was selected for an experimental <a href="/wiki/Ability_grouping" class="mw-redirect" title="Ability grouping">fast stream</a> education programme, which took him two years early to <a href="/wiki/Kirkcaldy_High_School" title="Kirkcaldy High School">Kirkcaldy High School</a> for an academic <a href="/wiki/Hothousing" title="Hothousing">hothouse education</a> taught in separate classes.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Aged 16, he wrote that he loathed and resented this "ludicrous" experiment on young lives.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>He was accepted by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> to study history at the same early age of 16. During an end-of-term <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a> match at his old school, he received a kick to the head and suffered a <a href="/wiki/Retinal_detachment" title="Retinal detachment">retinal detachment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> This left him <a href="/wiki/Blindness" class="mw-redirect" title="Blindness">blind</a> in his left eye, despite treatment including several operations and weeks spent lying in a darkened room. Later at Edinburgh, while playing tennis, he noticed the same symptoms in his right eye. Brown underwent experimental surgery at the <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Royal_Infirmary" class="mw-redirect" title="Edinburgh Royal Infirmary">Edinburgh Royal Infirmary</a> and his right eye was saved by a young eye surgeon, Hector Chawla.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ddd_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ddd-21">[21]</a></sup> Brown graduated from Edinburgh with a <a href="/wiki/First-Class_Honours" class="mw-redirect" title="First-Class Honours">First-Class Honours</a> <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Arts_(Scotland)" title="Master of Arts (Scotland)">MA</a> degree in history in 1972, and stayed on to obtain his <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy" title="Doctor of Philosophy">PhD</a> in history<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> (which he gained ten years later in 1982), titled <i>The Labour Party and Political Change in Scotland 1918–29</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbb_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbb-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In his youth at the University of Edinburgh, Brown was involved in a romantic relationship with <a href="/wiki/Princess_Margareta_of_Romania" class="mw-redirect" title="Princess Margareta of Romania">Margarita, Crown Princess of Romania</a>. Margarita said about it: "It was a very solid and romantic story. I never stopped loving him but one day it didn't seem right any more, it was politics, politics, politics, and I needed nurturing." An unnamed friend of those years is quoted by Paul Routledge in his biography of Brown as recalling: "She was sweet and gentle and obviously cut out to make somebody a very good wife. She was bright, too, though not like him, but they seemed made for each other."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1972, while still a student, Brown was elected <a href="/wiki/Rector_of_the_University_of_Edinburgh" title="Rector of the University of Edinburgh">Rector of the University of Edinburgh</a>, the convener of the <a href="/wiki/University_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="University Court">University Court</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> He served as Rector until 1975, and also edited the document <i>The Red Paper on Scotland</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Career_before_Parliament">Career before Parliament</span></h3> 
      <p>From 1976 to 1980 Brown was employed as a lecturer in politics at <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Caledonian_University" title="Glasgow Caledonian University">Glasgow College of Technology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> He also worked as a tutor for the <a href="/wiki/Open_University" title="Open University">Open University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/1979_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1979 United Kingdom general election">1979 general election</a>, Brown stood for the <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_South_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Edinburgh South (UK Parliament constituency)">Edinburgh South</a> constituency, losing to the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> candidate, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ancram" title="Michael Ancram">Michael Ancram</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbb_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbb-23">[23]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>From 1980, he worked as a journalist at <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Television" title="Scottish Television">Scottish Television</a>, later serving as current affairs editor until his election to Parliament in 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-GBStory_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GBStory-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_to_Parliament_and_opposition">Election to Parliament and opposition</span></h2> 
      <p>Brown was elected to Parliament as a Labour MP at his second attempt, for <a href="/wiki/Dunfermline_East_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Dunfermline East (UK Parliament constituency)">Dunfermline East</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1983_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1983 United Kingdom general election">1983 general election</a>. His first Westminster office mate was a newly elected MP from the Sedgefield constituency, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>. Brown became an <a href="/wiki/Opposition_(parliamentary)" class="mw-redirect" title="Opposition (parliamentary)">opposition</a> spokesman on Trade and Industry in 1985. In 1986, he published a biography of the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Labour_Party" title="Independent Labour Party">Independent Labour Party</a> politician <a href="/wiki/James_Maxton" title="James Maxton">James Maxton</a>, the subject of his doctoral thesis. Brown was <a href="/wiki/Shadow_Cabinet" title="Shadow Cabinet">Shadow</a> <a href="/wiki/Chief_Secretary_to_the_Treasury" title="Chief Secretary to the Treasury">Chief Secretary to the Treasury</a> from 1987 to 1989 and then Shadow <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Trade_and_Industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Trade and Industry">Secretary of State for Trade and Industry</a>, before becoming Shadow Chancellor in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-bbb_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbb-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GBStory_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GBStory-30">[30]</a></sup> Having led the <i>Labour Movement Yes</i> campaign, refusing to join the cross-party <i>Yes for Scotland</i> campaign, during the <a href="/wiki/1979_Scottish_devolution_referendum" title="1979 Scottish devolution referendum">1979 Scottish devolution referendum</a>, while other senior Labour politicians – including <a href="/wiki/Robin_Cook" title="Robin Cook">Robin Cook</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tam_Dalyell" title="Tam Dalyell">Tam Dalyell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Wilson_(Labour_politician)" title="Brian Wilson (Labour politician)">Brian Wilson</a> – campaigned for a <i>No</i> vote, Brown was subsequently a key participant in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Constitutional_Convention" title="Scottish Constitutional Convention">Scottish Constitutional Convention</a>, signing the <a href="/wiki/Claim_of_Right_1989" title="Claim of Right 1989">Claim of Right for Scotland</a> in 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Labour leader <a href="/wiki/John_Smith_(Labour_Party_leader)" title="John Smith (Labour Party leader)">John Smith</a> died suddenly in May 1994. Brown did not contest the leadership after <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> became the favourite, deciding to make way for Blair to avoid splitting the pro-modernising vote in the leadership ballot.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>It has long been rumoured <a href="/wiki/Blair%E2%80%93Brown_deal" title="Blair–Brown deal">a deal</a> was struck between Blair and Brown at the former Granita restaurant in <a href="/wiki/A1_in_London#Islington" title="A1 in London">Islington</a>, in which Blair promised to give Brown control of economic policy in return for Brown not standing against him in the <a href="/wiki/1994_Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election" class="mw-redirect" title="1994 Labour Party (UK) leadership election">leadership election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Whether this is true or not, the relationship between Blair and Brown was central to the fortunes of <a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a>, and they mostly remained united in public, despite reported serious private rifts.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As Shadow Chancellor, Brown as Chancellor-in-waiting was seen as a good choice by business and the middle class. While he was Chancellor inflation sometimes exceeded the 2% target causing the <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_the_Bank_of_England" title="Governor of the Bank of England">Governor of the Bank of England</a> to write several letters to the Chancellor, each time inflation exceeded three per cent.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Following a reorganisation of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Westminster_constituencies_from_2005" title="Scottish Westminster constituencies from 2005">Westminster constituencies</a> in Scotland in 2005, Brown became MP for <a href="/wiki/Kirkcaldy_and_Cowdenbeath_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Kirkcaldy and Cowdenbeath (UK Parliament constituency)">Kirkcaldy and Cowdenbeath</a> at the <a href="/wiki/2005_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2005 United Kingdom general election">general election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span id="Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer_.281997.E2.80.932007.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer_(1997–2007)">Chancellor of the Exchequer (1997–2007)</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: 
       <a href="/wiki/Chancellorship_of_Gordon_Brown" class="mw-redirect" title="Chancellorship of Gordon Brown">Chancellorship of Gordon Brown</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/1997_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1997 United Kingdom general election">1997 general election</a>, Labour defeated the Conservatives by a landslide to end their 18-year exile from government, and when <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>, the new Prime Minister, announced his <a href="/wiki/Blair_ministry" title="Blair ministry">ministerial team</a> on 2 May 1997, he appointed Brown as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>. Brown would remain in this role for 10 years and two months, making him the longest-serving Chancellor in modern history.<sup id="cite_ref-ddd_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ddd-21">[21]</a></sup> The Prime Minister's website highlights some achievements from Brown's decade as Chancellor: making the Bank of England independent and delivering an agreement on poverty and climate change at the <a href="/wiki/G8" class="mw-redirect" title="G8">G8</a> summit in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-bbb_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbb-23">[23]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gordon_Brown_IMF.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Gordon Brown standing at a podium. Text on the podium states &quot;ANNUAL MEETINGS&quot;. A number of flags hang in the background" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Gordon_Brown_IMF.jpg/220px-Gordon_Brown_IMF.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Gordon_Brown_IMF.jpg/330px-Gordon_Brown_IMF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Gordon_Brown_IMF.jpg/440px-Gordon_Brown_IMF.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1532"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gordon_Brown_IMF.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Gordon Brown speaking at the annual 
         <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>/
         <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">IMF</a> meeting in 2002
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_economic_reforms">Early economic reforms</span></h3> 
      <p>On taking office as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> Brown gave the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> operational independence in <a href="/wiki/Monetary_policy" title="Monetary policy">monetary policy</a>, and thus responsibility for setting <a href="/wiki/Interest_rate" title="Interest rate">interest rates</a> through the Bank's <a href="/wiki/Monetary_Policy_Committee" title="Monetary Policy Committee">Monetary Policy Committee</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> At the same time, he also changed the <a href="/wiki/Inflation_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Inflation rate">inflation measure</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Retail_Price_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="Retail Price Index">Retail Price Index</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Price_Index_(United_Kingdom)" title="Consumer Price Index (United Kingdom)">Consumer Price Index</a> and transferred responsibility for banking supervision to the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Services_Authority" title="Financial Services Authority">Financial Services Authority</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Some commentators have argued that this division of responsibilities exacerbated the severity in Britain of the 2007 global banking crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation_and_spending">Taxation and spending</span></h3> 
      <p>In the 1997 election and subsequently, Brown pledged not to increase the basic or higher rates of <a href="/wiki/Income_tax" title="Income tax">income tax</a>. Over his Chancellorship, he reduced the basic rate from 23% to 20%. However, in all but his final budget, Brown increased the tax thresholds in line with inflation, rather than earnings, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_drag" title="Fiscal drag">fiscal drag</a>. <a href="/wiki/Corporation_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Corporation tax">Corporation tax</a> fell under Brown, from a main rate of 33% to 28%, and from 24% to 19% for <a href="/wiki/Small_business" title="Small business">small businesses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AdamBrowne_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AdamBrowne-46">[46]</a></sup> In 1999, he introduced a lower <a href="/wiki/10p_tax_rate" title="10p tax rate">tax band of 10%</a>. He abolished this 10% tax band in <a href="/wiki/Alistair_Darling#10%_income_tax_band" title="Alistair Darling">his last budget</a> in 2007 to reduce the basic rate from 22% to 20%, increasing tax for 5 million people<sup id="cite_ref-indy_10revolt_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indy_10revolt-47">[47]</a></sup> and, according to the calculations of the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Fiscal_Studies" title="Institute for Fiscal Studies">Institute for Fiscal Studies</a>, leaving those earning between £5,000 and £18,000 as the biggest losers.<sup id="cite_ref-times_10criticism_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-times_10criticism-48">[48]</a></sup> To backbench cheers, Brown had described the measure in his last Budget thus: "Having put in place more focused ways of incentivising work and directly supporting children and pensioners at a cost of £3bn a year, I can now return income tax to just two rates by removing the 10p band on non savings income".<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>According to the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> UK taxation increased from a 39.3% share of gross domestic product in 1997 to 42.4% in 2006, going to a higher level than that of Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> This increase has mainly been attributed to active government policy, and not simply to the growing economy. Conservatives have accused Brown of imposing "<a href="/wiki/Stealth_tax" title="Stealth tax">stealth taxes</a>". A commonly reported example resulted in 1997 from a technical change in the way <a href="/wiki/Corporation_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Corporation tax">corporation tax</a> is collected, the indirect effect of which was for the <a href="/wiki/Dividend" title="Dividend">dividends</a> on <a href="/wiki/Stock" title="Stock">stock</a> investments held within pensions to be taxed, thus lowering pension returns and contributing to the demise of most of the final salary pension funds in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The Treasury contends that this tax change was crucial to long-term economic growth. </p>
      <p>Brown's 2000 Spending Review outlined a major expansion of <a href="/wiki/Government_spending" title="Government spending">government spending</a>, particularly on health and education. In his April 2002 budget, Brown increased <a href="/wiki/National_Insurance" title="National Insurance">National Insurance</a> to pay for health spending. He also introduced <a href="/wiki/Working_tax_credit" class="mw-redirect" title="Working tax credit">working tax credits</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and in his last budget as Chancellor, Brown gave an extra £3&nbsp;billion in pension allowances, an increase in the child tax credit, and an increase in the working tax credit. These increases were followed by another £1&nbsp;billion of support for increases in the child tax credit.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Under Brown, the tax code, the standard guide to tax, doubled in length to 17,000 pages.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_single_currency">European single currency</span></h3> 
      <p>In October 1997, Brown announced that the Treasury would set <a href="/wiki/Five_economic_tests" title="Five economic tests">five economic tests</a><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> to determine whether the economic case had been made for the United Kingdom to adopt the European single currency. The <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">Treasury</a> indicated that the tests had not been passed in June 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_issues">Other issues</span></h3> 
      <p>In 2000, Brown was accused of starting a political row about higher education (referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Laura_Spence_Affair" title="Laura Spence Affair">Laura Spence Affair</a>) when he accused the University of Oxford of elitism in its admissions procedures, describing its decision not to offer a place to <a href="/wiki/State_school" title="State school">state school</a> pupil Laura Spence as "absolutely outrageous".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roy_Jenkins" title="Roy Jenkins">Lord Jenkins</a>, then Oxford <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_(education)" title="Chancellor (education)">Chancellor</a> and himself a former Labour Chancellor of the Exchequer, said "nearly every fact he used was false."<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Between 1999 and 2002 Brown sold 60% of the UK's <a href="/wiki/Gold_reserve" title="Gold reserve">gold reserves</a> shortly before gold entered a protracted bull market, since nicknamed by dealers as the <a href="/wiki/Brown_Bottom" class="mw-redirect" title="Brown Bottom">Brown Bottom</a> or Brown's Bottom.<sup id="cite_ref-timesgold_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timesgold-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The official reason for selling the gold reserves was to reduce the <a href="/wiki/Financial_risk" title="Financial risk">portfolio risk</a> of the UK's reserves by diversifying away from gold.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The UK eventually sold about 395 tons of gold over 17 auctions from July 1999 to March 2002, at an average price of about US$275 per ounce, raising approximately US$3.5&nbsp;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> By 2011, that quantity of gold would be worth over $19 billion, leading to Brown's decision to sell the gold being widely criticised.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As Chancellor, Brown argued against <a href="/wiki/Renationalisation_of_British_Rail" class="mw-redirect" title="Renationalisation of British Rail">renationalising the railways</a>, saying at the Labour conference in 2004 that it would cost £22 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During his time as Chancellor, Brown reportedly believed that it was appropriate to remove most, but not all, of the unpayable <a href="/wiki/Third_World_debt" class="mw-redirect" title="Third World debt">Third World debt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> On 20 April 2006, in a speech to the United Nations Ambassadors, Brown outlined a "<a href="/wiki/Green_politics" title="Green politics">Green</a>" view of global development.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Run-up_to_succeeding_Tony_Blair">Run-up to succeeding Tony Blair</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: 
       <a href="/wiki/2007_Labour_Party_leadership_election_(UK)" title="2007 Labour Party leadership election (UK)">2007 Labour Party leadership election (UK)</a> and 
       <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_2007_Labour_Party_leadership_election_(UK)" title="Timeline of the 2007 Labour Party leadership election (UK)">Timeline of the 2007 Labour Party leadership election (UK)</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In October 2004, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> announced he would not lead the party into a fourth general election, but would serve a full third term.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Political comment over the relationship between Brown and Blair continued up to and beyond the <a href="/wiki/2005_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2005 United Kingdom general election">2005 election</a>, which Labour won with a reduced <a href="/wiki/Majority_government" title="Majority government">majority</a> and reduced vote share. Blair announced on 7 September 2006 that he would step down within a year.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Brown was the clear favourite to succeed Blair; he was the only <a href="/wiki/2007_Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election" class="mw-redirect" title="2007 Labour Party (UK) leadership election">candidate</a> spoken of seriously in Westminster. Appearances and news coverage leading up to the handover were interpreted as preparing the ground for Brown to become <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a>, in part by creating the impression of a statesman with a vision for leadership and <a href="/wiki/Global_change" title="Global change">global change</a>. This enabled Brown to signal the most significant priorities for his agenda as Prime Minister; speaking at a <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Society" title="Fabian Society">Fabian Society</a> conference on 'The Next Decade' in January 2007, he stressed education, international development, narrowing inequalities (to pursue 'equality of opportunity and fairness of outcome'), renewing Britishness, restoring trust in politics, and winning hearts and minds in the war on terror as key priorities.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span id="Prime_Minister_.282007.E2.80.9310.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Prime_Minister_(2007–10)">Prime Minister (2007–10)</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: 
       <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Gordon_Brown" title="Premiership of Gordon Brown">Premiership of Gordon Brown</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: 
       <a href="/wiki/Brown_ministry" title="Brown ministry">Brown ministry</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:George_W._and_Laura_Bush_%2B_Gordon_and_Sarah_Brown_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/George_W._and_Laura_Bush_%2B_Gordon_and_Sarah_Brown_2008.jpg/240px-George_W._and_Laura_Bush_%2B_Gordon_and_Sarah_Brown_2008.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/George_W._and_Laura_Bush_%2B_Gordon_and_Sarah_Brown_2008.jpg/360px-George_W._and_Laura_Bush_%2B_Gordon_and_Sarah_Brown_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fc/George_W._and_Laura_Bush_%2B_Gordon_and_Sarah_Brown_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="477" data-file-height="328"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:George_W._and_Laura_Bush_%2B_Gordon_and_Sarah_Brown_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Gordon Brown and President of the United States 
         <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> meet at Downing Street
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Brown ceased to be Chancellor and became the Prime Minister of the United Kingdom on 27 June 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Like all modern Prime Ministers, Brown concurrently served as the <a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Treasury" title="First Lord of the Treasury">First Lord of the Treasury</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_the_Civil_Service" title="Minister for the Civil Service">Minister for the Civil Service</a>, and was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">Cabinet of the United Kingdom</a>. Until his resignation from the post in May 2010 he was <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Labour Party (UK)">Leader of the Labour Party</a>. He was Member of Parliament for the constituency of <a href="/wiki/Kirkcaldy_and_Cowdenbeath_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Kirkcaldy and Cowdenbeath (UK Parliament constituency)">Kirkcaldy and Cowdenbeath</a> until he stepped down in 2015. He was the sixth post-war Prime Minister, of a total of 13, to assume the role without having won a general election.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Brown was the first Prime Minister from a Scottish constituency since the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> <a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Sir Alec Douglas-Home</a> in 1964. Not all British prime ministers have been university graduates, but, of those that were, Brown was one of only five that had not attended either Oxford or Cambridge.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Brown proposed moving some traditional prime ministerial powers conferred by <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">royal prerogative</a> to the realm of Parliament, such as the power to declare war and approve appointments to senior positions. Brown wanted Parliament to gain the right to ratify treaties and have more oversight into the intelligence services. He also proposed moving some powers from Parliament to citizens, including the right to form "citizens' juries", easily petition Parliament for new laws, and rally outside Westminster. He asserted that the attorney general should not have the right to decide whether to prosecute in individual cases, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Cash_for_Honours" class="mw-redirect" title="Cash for Honours">loans for peerages</a> scandal.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There was speculation during September and early October 2007 about whether Brown would call a <a href="/wiki/Snap_election" title="Snap election">snap general election</a>. Indeed, the party launched the <a href="/wiki/Not_Flash,_Just_Gordon" title="Not Flash, Just Gordon">Not Flash, Just Gordon</a> advertising campaign, which was seen largely as pre-election promotion of Brown as Prime Minister. However, Brown announced on 6 October<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> that there would be no election any time soon – despite opinion polls showing that he was capable of winning an election should he call one. This proved to be a costly mistake, as during 2008 his party slid behind the Conservatives (led by <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>) in the polls. Disputes over political donations, a string of losses in local elections, and by-election losses in <a href="/wiki/Crewe" title="Crewe">Crewe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a> did himself and the government no favours either.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>His political opponents accused him of being indecisive, which Brown denied.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> In July 2008 Brown supported a <a href="/wiki/Counter-Terrorism_Act_2008" title="Counter-Terrorism Act 2008">new bill extending the pre-charge detention period to 42 days</a>. The bill was met with opposition on both sides of the House and backbench rebellion. In the end the bill passed by just 9 votes.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The House of Lords defeated the bill, with Lords characterising it as "fatally flawed, ill thought through and unnecessary", stating that "it seeks to further erode fundamental legal and civil rights".<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Brown was mentioned by the press in the expenses crisis for claiming for the payment of his cleaner. However, no wrongdoing was found and the Commons Authority did not pursue Brown over the claim. Meanwhile, the Commons Fees Office stated that a double payment for a £153 plumbing repair bill was a mistake on their part and that Brown had repaid it in full.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbckeydetails_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbckeydetails-84">[84]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policy">Domestic policy</span></h3> 
      <p>During his Labour leadership campaign Brown proposed some policy initiatives, which he called the <i>manifesto for change</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The manifesto included a clampdown on corruption and a new <a href="/wiki/Ministerial_Code" title="Ministerial Code">Ministerial Code</a>, which set out clear standards of behaviour for ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> He also stated in a speech when announcing his bid that he wants a "better constitution" that is "clear about the rights and responsibilities of being a citizen in Britain today". He planned to set up an all-party convention to look at new powers for Parliament and to look at rebalancing powers between <a href="/wiki/Whitehall" title="Whitehall">Whitehall</a> and local government. Brown said he would give Parliament the final say on whether British troops were sent into action in future. </p>
      <p>He said he wanted to release more land and ease access to ownership with shared equity schemes. He backed a proposal to build new <a href="/wiki/Eco-towns" title="Eco-towns">eco-towns</a>, each housing between 10,000 and 20,000 home-owners – up to 100,000 new homes in total. Brown also said he wanted to have doctors' surgeries open at the weekends, and GPs on call in the evenings. Doctors were given the right of opting out of out-of-hours care in 2007, under a controversial pay deal, signed by then-Health Secretary <a href="/wiki/John_Reid,_Baron_Reid_of_Cardowan" title="John Reid, Baron Reid of Cardowan">John Reid</a>, which awarded them a 22 percent pay rise in 2006. Brown also stated in the manifesto that the NHS was his top priority. </p>
      <p>On 5 June 2007, just three weeks before he was due to take the post of Prime Minister, Brown made a speech promising "British Jobs for British workers".<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Brown reiterated that promise at the Labour Party's annual conference in September, which caused controversy as he coupled this with a commitment to crack down on migrant workers.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBCJobs_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCJobs-90">[90]</a></sup> The Conservative Party, led by <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, promptly pointed out that such a commitment was illegal under EU law.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCJobs_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCJobs-90">[90]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gordon_Brown_troop_visit.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Gordon_Brown_troop_visit.jpg/220px-Gordon_Brown_troop_visit.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Gordon_Brown_troop_visit.jpg/330px-Gordon_Brown_troop_visit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Gordon_Brown_troop_visit.jpg/440px-Gordon_Brown_troop_visit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="629"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gordon_Brown_troop_visit.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Brown meets British troops during a visit to Basra, 2007
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Obama,_Prince_Charles,_Brown,_Harper_%26_Sarkozy_at_Normandy_American_Cemetery_and_Memorial_2009-06-06.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Obama%2C_Prince_Charles%2C_Brown%2C_Harper_%26_Sarkozy_at_Normandy_American_Cemetery_and_Memorial_2009-06-06.JPG/220px-Obama%2C_Prince_Charles%2C_Brown%2C_Harper_%26_Sarkozy_at_Normandy_American_Cemetery_and_Memorial_2009-06-06.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Obama%2C_Prince_Charles%2C_Brown%2C_Harper_%26_Sarkozy_at_Normandy_American_Cemetery_and_Memorial_2009-06-06.JPG/330px-Obama%2C_Prince_Charles%2C_Brown%2C_Harper_%26_Sarkozy_at_Normandy_American_Cemetery_and_Memorial_2009-06-06.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Obama%2C_Prince_Charles%2C_Brown%2C_Harper_%26_Sarkozy_at_Normandy_American_Cemetery_and_Memorial_2009-06-06.JPG/440px-Obama%2C_Prince_Charles%2C_Brown%2C_Harper_%26_Sarkozy_at_Normandy_American_Cemetery_and_Memorial_2009-06-06.JPG 2x" data-file-width="525" data-file-height="350"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Obama,_Prince_Charles,_Brown,_Harper_%26_Sarkozy_at_Normandy_American_Cemetery_and_Memorial_2009-06-06.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>US President 
         <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>, 
         <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">Prince Charles</a>, Brown, Canadian Prime Minister 
         <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Harper" title="Stephen Harper">Stephen Harper</a> and French President 
         <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a> arrive at the Colleville-sur-Mer cemetery to attend a ceremony marking the 65th anniversary of the 
         <a href="/wiki/D-Day_landings" class="mw-redirect" title="D-Day landings">D-Day landings</a> in 
         <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a>, 6 June 2009.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Brown was committed to the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>, but said in a speech in June 2007 that he would "learn the lessons" from the mistakes made in Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Brown said in a letter published on 17 March 2008 that the United Kingdom would hold an inquiry into the war.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Brown went to great lengths to empathise with those who lost family members in the Iraq and Afghanistan conflicts. He has often said "War is tragic", echoing Blair's quote, "War is horrible".<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Nonetheless, in November 2007 Brown was accused by some senior military figures of not adhering to the <a href="/wiki/Military_Covenant" class="mw-redirect" title="Military Covenant">Military Covenant</a>, a convention within British politics ensuring adequate safeguards, rewards and compensation for military personnel who risk their lives in obedience to orders derived from the policy of the elected government.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Brown did not attend the opening ceremony of the <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics" title="2008 Summer Olympics">2008 Summer Olympics</a> on 8 August 2008 in Beijing; instead, he attended the closing ceremony on 24 August 2008. Brown had been under intense pressure from human rights campaigners to send a message to China, concerning the <a href="/wiki/2008_Tibetan_unrest" title="2008 Tibetan unrest">2008 Tibetan unrest</a>. His decision not to attend the opening ceremony was not an act of protest, but rather was made several weeks in advance and not intended as a stand on principle.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In a speech in July 2007, Brown clarified his position regarding Britain's relationship with the USA<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> "We will not allow people to separate us from the United States of America in dealing with the common challenges that we face around the world. I think people have got to remember that the special relationship between a British prime minister and an American president is built on the things that we share, the same enduring values about the importance of liberty, opportunity, the dignity of the individual. I will continue to work, as <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> did, very closely with the American administration." </p>
      <p>Brown and the Labour party had pledged to allow a referendum on the EU <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Treaty of Lisbon</a>. On 13 December 2007, Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/David_Miliband" title="David Miliband">David Miliband</a> attended for the Prime Minister at the official signing ceremony in Lisbon of the <a href="/wiki/EU_Reform_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="EU Reform Treaty">EU Reform Treaty</a>. Brown's opponents on both sides of the House, and in the press, suggested that ratification by Parliament was not enough and that a referendum should also be held. Labour's 2005 manifesto had pledged to give British public a referendum on the original <a href="/wiki/EU_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="EU Constitution">EU Constitution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Brown argued that the Treaty significantly differed from the Constitution, and as such did not require a referendum. He also responded with plans for a lengthy debate on the topic, and stated that he believed the document to be too complex to be decided by referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drug_policy">Drug policy</span></h3> 
      <p>During Brown's premiership, in October 2008, the <a href="/wiki/Advisory_Council_on_the_Misuse_of_Drugs" title="Advisory Council on the Misuse of Drugs">Advisory Council on the Misuse of Drugs</a> (ACMD) recommended to the then <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Jacqui_Smith" title="Jacqui Smith">Jacqui Smith</a> that <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">cannabis</a> remain classified as a <a href="/wiki/Class_C_drugs" class="mw-redirect" title="Class C drugs">Class C drug</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_cannabis_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_cannabis-100">[100]</a></sup> Acting against the advice of the Council, she chose to reclassify it as <a href="/wiki/Drugs_controlled_by_the_UK_Misuse_of_Drugs_Act#Class_B_drugs" title="Drugs controlled by the UK Misuse of Drugs Act">Class B</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_cannabis_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News_cannabis-100">[100]</a></sup> After Professor <a href="/wiki/David_Nutt" title="David Nutt">David Nutt</a>, the chair of the ACMD, criticised this move in a lecture in 2009, he was asked to step down by then Home Secretary <a href="/wiki/Alan_Johnson" title="Alan Johnson">Alan Johnson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Following his resignation, Professor Nutt said Brown had "made up his mind" to reclassify cannabis despite evidence to the contrary.<sup id="cite_ref-Batty20091031_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Batty20091031-102">[102]</a></sup> Brown had argued, "I don't think that the previous studies took into account that so much of the cannabis on the streets is now of a lethal quality and we really have got to send out a message to young people—this is not acceptable".<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Professor Nutt's predecessor at the ACMD, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Rawlins" title="Michael Rawlins">Sir Michael Rawlins</a>, later said, "Governments may well have good reasons for taking an alternative view&nbsp;... When that happens, then the government should explain why it's ignoring the particular advice".<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_recession">Global recession</span></h3> 
      <p>Brown's premiership coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">global recession</a>, during which Brown called for fiscal action in an attempt to stimulate aggregate demand. Domestically, Brown's administration introduced measures including a <a href="/wiki/2008_United_Kingdom_bank_rescue_package" title="2008 United Kingdom bank rescue package">bank rescue package</a> worth around £500&nbsp;billion (approximately $850&nbsp;billion), a temporary 2.5 percentage point cut in <a href="/wiki/Value-added_tax" title="Value-added tax">value-added tax</a><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and a "car scrappage" scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plots_against_leadership">Plots against leadership</span></h3> 
      <p>In mid-2008, Brown's leadership was presented with a challenge as some MPs openly called for him to resign. This event was dubbed the 'Lancashire Plot', as two backbenchers from <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1972" title="Local Government Act 1972">(pre-1974) Lancashire</a> urged him to step down and a third questioned his chances of holding on to the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> leadership. Several MPs argued that if Brown did not recover in the polls by early 2009, he should call for a leadership contest. However, certain prominent MPs, such as <a href="/wiki/Jacqui_Smith" title="Jacqui Smith">Jacqui Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Rammell" title="Bill Rammell">Bill Rammell</a>, suggested that Brown was the right person to lead Britain through its economic crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> In the autumn, <a href="/wiki/Siobhain_McDonagh" title="Siobhain McDonagh">Siobhain McDonagh</a>, a MP and junior government whip, who during her time in office had never voted against the government,<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> spoke of the need for discussion over Brown's position. While she did not state that she wanted Brown deposed, she implored the Labour Party to hold a leadership election. McDonagh was sacked from her role shortly afterward, on 12 September.<sup id="cite_ref-Whipsacked_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whipsacked-110">[110]</a></sup> She was supported in making clear her desire for a contest by <a href="/wiki/Joan_Ryan" title="Joan Ryan">Joan Ryan</a> (who applied, as McDonagh had, for leadership nomination papers, and became the second rebel to be fired from her job), <a href="/wiki/Jim_Dowd_(politician)" title="Jim Dowd (politician)">Jim Dowd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greg_Pope" title="Greg Pope">Greg Pope</a>, and a string of others who had previously held positions in government.<sup id="cite_ref-Exministers_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exministers-111">[111]</a></sup> In the face of this speculation over Brown's future, his ministers backed him to lead the party, and <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Harman" title="Harriet Harman">Harriet Harman</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Miliband" title="David Miliband">David Miliband</a> denied that they were preparing leadership bids. After Labour lost the <a href="/wiki/2008_Glasgow_East_by-election" title="2008 Glasgow East by-election">Glasgow East by-election</a> in July, Harman, the deputy leader of the party, said that Brown was the "solution", not the "problem"; Home Secretary Smith, Justice Secretary <a href="/wiki/Jack_Straw" title="Jack Straw">Jack Straw</a>, Schools Secretary <a href="/wiki/Ed_Balls" title="Ed Balls">Ed Balls</a> and Cabinet Office Minister <a href="/wiki/Ed_Miliband" title="Ed Miliband">Ed Miliband</a> all re-affirmed their support for Brown.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The deputy Prime Minister under Blair, <a href="/wiki/John_Prescott" title="John Prescott">John Prescott</a>, also pledged his support.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/David_Miliband" title="David Miliband">David Miliband</a> then denied that he was plotting a leadership bid, when on 30 July, an article written by him in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> was interpreted by a large number in the media as an attempt to undermine Brown. In the article, Miliband outlined the party's future, but neglected to mention the Prime Minister. Miliband, responded to this by saying that he was confident Brown could lead Labour to victory in the next general election, and that his article was an attack against the <a href="/wiki/Fatalism" title="Fatalism">fatalism</a> in the party since the loss of Glasgow-East.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Miliband continued to show his support for Brown in the face of the challenge that emerged in September, as did Business Secretary <a href="/wiki/John_Hutton,_Baron_Hutton_of_Furness" title="John Hutton, Baron Hutton of Furness">John Hutton</a>, Environment Secretary <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Benn" title="Hilary Benn">Hilary Benn</a>, and Chief Whip <a href="/wiki/Geoff_Hoon" title="Geoff Hoon">Geoff Hoon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 6 January 2010, <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Hewitt" title="Patricia Hewitt">Patricia Hewitt</a> and Geoff Hoon jointly called for a secret ballot on the future of Brown's leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The call received little support, and the following day Hoon said that it appeared to have failed and was "over". Brown later referred to the call for a secret ballot as a "form of silliness".<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By-elections_and_2009_local_and_EU_elections">By-elections and 2009 local and EU elections</span></h3> 
      <p>In the local elections on 1 May 2008, Labour suffered its worst results in 40 years, finishing in third place with a projected 24% share of the national vote.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Subsequently, the party saw the loss of by-elections in <a href="/wiki/2008_Crewe_and_Nantwich_by-election" title="2008 Crewe and Nantwich by-election">Nantwich and Crewe</a> and <a href="/wiki/2008_Henley_by-election" title="2008 Henley by-election">Henley</a> as well as slumps in the polls. A <a href="/wiki/2008_Glasgow_East_by-election" title="2008 Glasgow East by-election">by-election in Glasgow East</a> triggered by the resignation of <a href="/wiki/David_Marshall_(British_politician)" title="David Marshall (British politician)">David Marshall</a> saw Labour struggle to appoint a candidate, eventually settling for <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Curran" title="Margaret Curran">Margaret Curran</a>, a sitting MSP in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a>. The SNP, Conservatives and Liberal Democrats all derided Labour for their disorganised nature, with <a href="/wiki/Alex_Salmond" title="Alex Salmond">Alex Salmond</a> commenting "This is their 'lost weekend'—they don't have a leader in Scotland, they don't have a candidate in Glasgow East, and they have a prime minister who refuses to come to the constituency".<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Labour lost the constituency to the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a>'s <a href="/wiki/John_Mason_(Scottish_politician)" title="John Mason (Scottish politician)">John Mason</a> who took 11,277 votes, with Labour just 365 behind. The seat experienced a swing of 22.54%.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/2009_European_Parliament_election_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="2009 European Parliament election in the United Kingdom">European elections</a>, Labour polled 16% of the vote, finishing in third place behind the Conservatives and <a href="/wiki/UK_Independence_Party" title="UK Independence Party">UK Independence Party</a> (UKIP).<sup id="cite_ref-labour_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-labour-6">[6]</a></sup> Voter apathy was reflected in the historically low turnout of around thirty three percent. In Scotland voter turnout was only twenty eight per cent. In the <a href="/wiki/2009_United_Kingdom_local_elections" title="2009 United Kingdom local elections">local elections</a>, Labour polled 23% of the vote, finishing in third place behind <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>, with Labour losing control of the four councils it had held prior to the election.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> In a vote widely considered to be a reaction to the expenses scandal, the share of the votes was down for all the major parties; Labour was down one percent, the Conservative share was down five percent. The beneficiary of the public backlash was generally seen to be the minor parties, including the <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_England_and_Wales" title="Green Party of England and Wales">Green Party</a> and UKIP. These results were Labour's worst since World War II. Brown was quoted in the press as having said that the results were "a painful defeat for Labour", and that "too many good people doing so much good for their communities and their constituencies have lost through no fault of their own."<sup id="cite_ref-timesonline1_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timesonline1-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_general_election">2010 general election</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Gordon_Brown,_University_of_Bradford.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Gordon_Brown%2C_University_of_Bradford.jpg/220px-Gordon_Brown%2C_University_of_Bradford.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Gordon_Brown%2C_University_of_Bradford.jpg/330px-Gordon_Brown%2C_University_of_Bradford.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Gordon_Brown%2C_University_of_Bradford.jpg/440px-Gordon_Brown%2C_University_of_Bradford.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3805" data-file-height="2880"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Gordon_Brown,_University_of_Bradford.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Gordon and Sarah Brown at the 
         <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bradford" title="University of Bradford">University of Bradford</a> the day before the 
         <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2010 United Kingdom general election">2010 election</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: 
       <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2010 United Kingdom general election">2010 United Kingdom general election</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In April 2010, Brown asked the Queen to dissolve Parliament. The general election campaign included the first televised leadership debates in Britain. The result of the election on 6 May was a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Brown was re-elected as MP for Kirkcaldy and Cowdenbeath with 29,559 votes.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resignation">Resignation</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: 
       <a href="/wiki/2010_Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election" class="mw-redirect" title="2010 Labour Party (UK) leadership election">2010 Labour Party (UK) leadership election</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Brown announced on 10 May 2010 that he would stand down as Labour Leader, with a view to a successor being chosen before the next <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour Party (UK) Conference">Labour Party Conference</a> in September 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-resignation_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-resignation-126">[126]</a></sup> The following day, negotiations between the Labour Party and the Liberal Democrats to form a coalition government failed. During the evening, Brown visited <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a> to tender his resignation as Prime Minister to <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> and to recommend that she invite the Leader of the Opposition, <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, to form a government.<sup id="cite_ref-PMRes_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PMRes-127">[127]</a></sup> He resigned as leader of the Labour Party with immediate effect.<sup id="cite_ref-PMRes_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PMRes-127">[127]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-premiership">Post-premiership</span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Accelerating_Infrastructure_Development_Gordon_Brown_(8412051140).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Accelerating_Infrastructure_Development_Gordon_Brown_%288412051140%29.jpg/170px-Accelerating_Infrastructure_Development_Gordon_Brown_%288412051140%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Accelerating_Infrastructure_Development_Gordon_Brown_%288412051140%29.jpg/255px-Accelerating_Infrastructure_Development_Gordon_Brown_%288412051140%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Accelerating_Infrastructure_Development_Gordon_Brown_%288412051140%29.jpg/340px-Accelerating_Infrastructure_Development_Gordon_Brown_%288412051140%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3400" data-file-height="5504"></a>

         <a href="/wiki/World_Economic_Forum" title="World Economic Forum">World Economic Forum</a>, January 2013.
